<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">History of the Middle East</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Aspect of history</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Middle_East.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="993" decoding="async" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_Middle_East.png/250px-Map_of_Middle_East.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_Middle_East.png/375px-Map_of_Middle_East.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_Middle_East.png/500px-Map_of_Middle_East.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Middle_East.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contemporary political map of the Middle East</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Middle_East_geographic.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="918" data-file-width="1026" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Middle_East_geographic.jpg/250px-Middle_East_geographic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Middle_East_geographic.jpg/375px-Middle_East_geographic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Middle_East_geographic.jpg/500px-Middle_East_geographic.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Middle_East_geographic.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map showing territories commonly considered part of the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a></div></div></div>
<p>Home to one of the <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_civilization" title="Cradle of civilization">Cradles of Civilization</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>—interchangeable with the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>—has seen many of the world's oldest cultures and civilizations. This history started from the earliest human settlements, continuing through several major pre- and post-Islamic Empires through to the nation-states of the Middle East today.
</p>

<span style="font-size: 120%;">↓</span> <a href="/wiki/Future" title="Future">Future</a> <span class="nowrap">  </span></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="background:#f8eaba;padding:0.4em;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_history" title="Template:Human history"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Human_history" title="Template talk:Human history"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Human_history&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a> were the first people to develop complex systems as to be called "Civilization", starting as far back as the 5th millennium BC. Egyptian civilization coalesced around 3150 BC with the political unification of <a href="/wiki/Upper_and_Lower_Egypt" title="Upper and Lower Egypt">Upper and Lower Egypt</a> under the first <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> was home to several powerful empires that came to rule almost the entire Middle East—particularly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Empire" title="Assyrian Empire">Assyrian Empires</a> of 1365–1076 BC and the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> of 911–609 BC. From the early 7th century BC and onward, the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> followed by the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> and other subsequent Iranian states and empires dominated the region. In the 1st century BC, the expanding <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> absorbed the whole <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a>, which included much of the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>. The Eastern Roman Empire, today commonly known as the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, ruling from the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>, became increasingly defined by and dogmatic about <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, gradually creating religious rifts between the doctrines dictated by the establishment in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and believers in many parts of the Middle East. From the 3rd century up to the course of the 7th century AD, the entire Middle East was dominated by the Byzantines and the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>. From the 7th century, a new power was rising in the Middle East, that of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. The dominance of the <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> came to a sudden end in the mid-11th century with the arrival of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuq_dynasty" title="Seljuq dynasty">Seljuq dynasty</a>. In the early 13th century, a new wave of invaders, the armies of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>, mainly <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a>, swept through the region. By the early 15th century, a new power had arisen in western Anatolia, the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> emirs, linguistically Turkic and religiously Islamic, who in <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">1453 captured the Christian Byzantine capital of Constantinople</a> and made themselves <a href="/wiki/List_of_sultans_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="List of sultans of the Ottoman Empire">sultans</a>.
</p><p>Large parts of the Middle East became a warground between the Ottomans and the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid dynasty</a> for centuries, starting in the early 16th century. By 1700, the Ottomans had been driven out of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary_(1526%E2%80%931867)" title="Kingdom of Hungary (1526–1867)">Kingdom of Hungary</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_power_(international_relations)" title="Balance of power (international relations)">balance of power</a> along the frontier had shifted decisively in favor of the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>. The <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> also established effective control of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French colonial empire</a> extended its influence into <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syria_(region)" title="Syria (region)">Syria</a>. In 1912, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Kingdom of Italy</a> seized <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dodecanese" title="Dodecanese">Dodecanese islands</a>, just off the coast of the Ottoman heartland of <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>. In the late 19th and early 20th centuries, Middle Eastern rulers tried to modernize their states to compete more effectively with the European powers. A turning point in the <b>history of the Middle East</b> came when <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> was discovered, first in Persia in 1908 and later in <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> (in 1938) and the other <a href="/wiki/Arab_states_of_the_Persian_Gulf" title="Arab states of the Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf states</a>, and also in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>. A Western dependence on Middle Eastern oil and the decline of British influence led to a growing American interest in the region.
</p><p>During the 1920s, 1930s, and 1940s, Syria and Egypt made moves towards independence. The British, the French, and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> departed from many parts of the Middle East during and after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> (1939–1945). The <a href="/wiki/Arab%E2%80%93Israeli_conflict" title="Arab–Israeli conflict">Arab–Israeli conflict</a> in <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> culminated in the 1947 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1947_UN_Partition_Plan" title="1947 UN Partition Plan">plan to partition Palestine</a>. Later in the midst of <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> tensions, the Arabic-speaking countries of <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Africa" title="Northern Africa">Northern Africa</a> saw the rise of <a href="/wiki/Pan-Arabism" title="Pan-Arabism">pan-Arabism</a>. The departure of the European powers from direct control of the region, the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, and the increasing importance of the <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry" title="Petroleum industry">petroleum industry</a>, marked the creation of the modern Middle East. In most Middle Eastern countries, the growth of market economies was inhibited by political restrictions, corruption and <a href="/wiki/Cronyism" title="Cronyism">cronyism</a>, overspending on arms and prestige projects, and over-dependence on oil revenues. The wealthiest economies in the region <a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per capita</a> are the small oil-rich countries of <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>: <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>.
</p><p>A combination of factors—among them the 1967 <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">Six-Day War</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wright-2">[2]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/1970s_energy_crisis" title="1970s energy crisis">1970s energy crisis</a> beginning with the 1973 <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> oil embargo in response to U.S. support of Israel in the <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">Yom Kippur War</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-wright-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> the concurrent Saudi-led popularization of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salafism" title="Salafism">Salafism</a>/<a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kepel-petro_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kepel-petro-4">[4]</a></sup> and the 1978-79 <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">Iranian Revolution</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>—promoted the increasing rise of <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamism</a> and the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Islamic_revival" title="Islamic revival">Islamic revival</a> (<i>Tajdid</i>). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Fall of the Soviet Union">Fall of the Soviet Union</a> in 1991 brought a global security refocus from the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" title="War on Terror">War on Terror</a>. Starting in the early 2010s, a <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_wave" title="Revolutionary wave">revolutionary wave</a> popularly known as the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a> brought major protests, uprisings, and revolutions to several Middle Eastern and <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> countries. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anbar_campaign_(2013%E2%80%9314)" title="Anbar campaign (2013–14)">Clashes in western Iraq</a> on 30 December 2013 were preliminary to the Sunni <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pan-Islamist" title="Pan-Islamist">pan-Islamist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">ISIL</a> uprising.
</p><p>The term <i>Near East</i> can be used interchangeably with <i>Middle East</i>, but in a different context, especially when discussing <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">ancient history</a>, it may have a limited meaning, namely the northern, historically <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a>-speaking <a href="/wiki/Semitic_people" title="Semitic people">Semitic people</a> area and adjacent <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolian</a> territories, marked in the two maps below.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:205px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NearEast3.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="182" data-file-width="203" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4b/NearEast3.png" width="203"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NearEast3.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#008000; color:white;"> </span> The limited modern archaeological and historical context of the Near East</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#00ff00; color:black;"> </span> Middle East and Near East</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Semitic_languages.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="917" data-file-width="712" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Semitic_languages.svg/220px-Semitic_languages.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Semitic_languages.svg/330px-Semitic_languages.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Semitic_languages.svg/440px-Semitic_languages.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Semitic_languages.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The historical <a href="/wiki/Semitic_people" title="Semitic people">Semitic region</a>, defined by the pre-Islamic distribution of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semitic_language" title="Semitic language">Semitic languages</a> and coinciding very roughly with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_plate" title="Arabian plate">Arabian plate</a>. Not so much lingually but rather culturally, politically and historically, the most significant division here has been between the north and the south, to some degree isolated from each other by the sparsely populated <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Desert" title="Arabian Desert">Arabian Desert</a>. The north comprises <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, which, together with the lower <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> (i.e., Egypt), constitute the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>.</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General">General</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: General">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Geographically, the Middle East can be thought of as <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> with the addition of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> (which is the non-<a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Africa" title="Northern Africa">Northern Africa</a>) and with the exclusion of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>. The Middle East was the first to experience a <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> (c. the 10th millennium BCE), as well as the first to enter the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> (c. 3300–1200 BC) and <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> (c. 1200–500 BC).
</p><p>Historically human populations have tended to settle around bodies of water, which is reflected in modern population density patterns. <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">Irrigation</a> systems were extremely important for the agricultural Middle East: for Egypt that of the lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_River" title="Nile River">Nile River</a>, and for Mesopotamia that of the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> rivers. Levantine agriculture depended on <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> rather than on the river-based irrigation of Egypt and Mesopotamia, resulting in preference for different crops. Since travel was faster and easier by sea, civilizations along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a> and later Greece, participated in intense trade. Similarly, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Yemen#Ancient_history" title="History of Yemen">Ancient Yemen</a>, much more conducive to agriculture than the rest of the Arabian Peninsula, sea traded heavily with the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>, some of which it lingually Semitized. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adnanite" title="Adnanite">Adnanite</a> Arabs, inhabiting the drier desert areas of the Middle East, were all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomadic" title="Nomadic">nomadic</a> <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralists</a> before some began settling in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_state" title="City state">city states</a>, with the geo-linguistic distribution today being divided between <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Najd" title="Najd">Najd</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a> in the Peninsula, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> areas beyond the Peninsula.
</p><p>Since ancient times the Middle East has had several <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a>: <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 14th–8th century BC), <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 8th century BC – 8th century AD),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 4th century BC – 8th century AD), and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 8th century AD – present). Familiarity with English is not uncommon among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Class" title="Middle Class">middle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Upper_class" title="Upper class">upper classes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Arabic is not commonly spoken in Turkey, Iran, and Israel, and some <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">varieties of Arabic</a> lack <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutual intelligibility</a>, thus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language#Language_and_dialect" title="Arabic language">qualifying as distinct languages</a> by this linguistic criterion.
</p><p>The Middle East was the birthplace of the <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnostic</a>, and most <a href="/wiki/Iranian_religions" title="Iranian religions">Iranian religions</a>. Initially the ancient inhabitants of the region followed various <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_religion" title="Ethnic religion">ethnic religions</a>, but most of those began to be gradually replaced at first by Christianity (even before the 313 AD <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Milan" title="Edict of Milan">Edict of Milan</a>) and finally by Islam (after the spread of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a> beyond the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> in 634 AD). To this day, however, the Middle East has, in particular, some <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_Middle_East" title="Christianity in the Middle East">sizable, ethnically distinct Christian minority groups</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> as well as Jews, concentrated in Israel, and followers of Iranian religions, such as <a href="/wiki/Yazd%C3%A2nism" title="Yazdânism">Yazdânism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>. Some of the smaller <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnoreligious" title="Ethnoreligious">ethnoreligious</a> minorities include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shabak_people" title="Shabak people">Shabak people</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mandaeans" title="Mandaeans">Mandaeans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Samaritans" title="Samaritans">Samaritans</a>. It is somewhat controversial whether the <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a> religion is a distinct religion in its own right or merely a part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ismailism" title="Ismailism">Ismailist</a> branch of <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_McLaurin_1979_114_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-De_McLaurin_1979_114-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_Near_East">Prehistoric Near East<span class="anchor" id="Prehistoric_Near_East"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Prehistoric Near East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Egypt" title="Prehistoric Egypt">Prehistoric Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_Iran" title="Prehistory of Iran">Prehistory of Iran</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_the_Southern_Levant" title="Prehistory of the Southern Levant">Prehistory of the Southern Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Cyprus" title="Prehistoric Cyprus">Prehistoric Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natufian_culture" title="Natufian culture">Natufian culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">History of agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Middle_Eastern_history" title="Timeline of Middle Eastern history">Timeline of Middle Eastern history</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_human_prehistory" title="Timeline of human prehistory">Timeline of human prehistory</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fertile_crescent_Neolithic_B_circa_7500_BC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1087" data-file-width="1050" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Fertile_crescent_Neolithic_B_circa_7500_BC.jpg/290px-Fertile_crescent_Neolithic_B_circa_7500_BC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Fertile_crescent_Neolithic_B_circa_7500_BC.jpg/435px-Fertile_crescent_Neolithic_B_circa_7500_BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Fertile_crescent_Neolithic_B_circa_7500_BC.jpg/580px-Fertile_crescent_Neolithic_B_circa_7500_BC.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fertile_crescent_Neolithic_B_circa_7500_BC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Area of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertile_crescent" title="Fertile crescent">fertile crescent</a>, circa 7500 BC, with main sites of the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Pottery_Neolithic" title="Pre-Pottery Neolithic">Pre-Pottery Neolithic</a> period.  The area of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> proper was not yet settled by humans.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Plate" title="Arabian Plate">Arabian Tectonic Plate</a> was part of the <a href="/wiki/African_Plate" title="African Plate">African Plate</a> during much of the <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a> Eon (<a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a>–<a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a>), until the <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> Epoch of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cenozoic_Era" title="Cenozoic Era">Cenozoic Era</a>. <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> rifting began in the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>, but the separation of Africa and Arabia occurred in the Oligocene, and since then the Arabian Plate has been slowly moving toward the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian Plate</a>.
</p><p>The collision between the Arabian Plate and Eurasia is pushing up the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a> of Iran. Because the Arabian Plate and Eurasia plate collide, many cities are in danger such as those in south eastern Turkey (which is on the Arabian Plate). These dangers include earthquakes, tsunamis, and volcanoes.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Early_human_migrations" title="Early human migrations">earliest human migrations</a> out of Africa occurred through the Middle East, namely over the <a href="/wiki/Levantine_corridor" title="Levantine corridor">Levantine corridor</a>, with the pre-modern <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> about 1.8 million years <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_present" title="Before present">BP</a>. One of the potential routes for early human migrations toward southern and eastern Asia is Iran.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haplogroup_J-P209" title="Haplogroup J-P209">Haplogroup J-P209</a>, the most common <a href="/wiki/Human_Y-chromosome_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroup">human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroup</a> in the Middle East today, is believed to have arisen in the region 31,700±12,800 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The two main current subgroups, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haplogroup_J1" title="Haplogroup J1">J-M267</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haplogroup_J2" title="Haplogroup J2">J-M172</a>, which now comprise between them almost all of the population of the haplogroup, are both believed to have arisen very early, at least 10,000 years ago. Nonetheless, Y-chromosomes <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_F-M89" title="Haplogroup F-M89">F-M89*</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_IJ" title="Haplogroup IJ">IJ-M429*</a> were reported to have been observed in the Iranian plateau.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grugni2012_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grugni2012-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is evidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_carvings" title="Rock carvings">rock carvings</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> terraces and in desert oases. In the <a href="/wiki/10th_millennium_BC" title="10th millennium BC">10th millennium BC</a>, a culture of <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishermen</a> was replaced by a <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">grain</a>-grinding culture. Climate changes and/or overgrazing around 6000 BC began to desiccate the pastoral lands of Egypt, forming the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>. Early tribal peoples migrated to the Nile River, where they developed a settled agricultural <a href="/wiki/Economic_system" title="Economic system">economy</a> and more centralized society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agriculturalists" title="Agriculturalists">agriculturalists</a>, who may have resided in <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Africa" title="Northeast Africa">Northeast Africa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, may have been the source population for <a href="/wiki/Lactase_persistence" title="Lactase persistence">lactase persistence</a> variants, including –13910*T, and may have been subsequently supplanted by later migrations of peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Priehodová_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Priehodová-17">[17]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan" title="Sub-Saharan">Sub-Saharan</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_African" title="West African">West African</a> Fulani, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_African" title="North African">North African</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuareg" title="Tuareg">Tuareg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Early_European_Farmers" title="Early European Farmers"> European agriculturalists</a>, who are descendants of these Neolithic agriculturalists, share the lactase persistence variant –13910*T.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Priehodová_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Priehodová-17">[17]</a></sup> While shared by Fulani and Tuareg herders, compared to the Tuareg variant, the Fulani variant of –13910*T has undergone a longer period of haplotype differentiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Priehodová_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Priehodová-17">[17]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fulani" title="Fulani">Fulani</a> lactase persistence variant –13910*T may have spread, along with cattle <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralism</a>, between 9686 BP and 7534 BP, possibly around 8500 BP; corroborating this timeframe for the Fulani, by at least 7500 BP, there is evidence of herders engaging in the act of <a href="/wiki/Milking" title="Milking">milking</a> in the Central <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Priehodová_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Priehodová-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Near_East">Ancient Near East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient Near East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Ancient Near East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">Ancient history</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Short_chronology" title="Short chronology">Short chronology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Egypt_Wings.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="109" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="38" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Ancient_Egypt_Wings.svg/350px-Ancient_Egypt_Wings.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Ancient_Egypt_Wings.svg/525px-Ancient_Egypt_Wings.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Ancient_Egypt_Wings.svg/700px-Ancient_Egypt_Wings.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Egypt_Wings.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The symbol of the <a href="/wiki/Winged_sun" title="Winged sun">winged sun</a> was found throughout the Middle East. It was associated with divinity, royalty, and power. The symbol shown above is an Egyptian version. The modern-day <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_flag" title="Assyrian flag">Assyrian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramean_flag" title="Aramean flag">Aramean flags</a> feature different versions of the symbol. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Israelite" title="Israelite">Israelite</a> royal <a href="/wiki/Winged_sun#Hebrew" title="Winged sun">Seals of Hezekiah</a> also featured one, sometimes flanked on either side with the Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Ankh" title="Ankh">ankh</a> symbol. The Iranian kingdom has a related symbol called <a href="/wiki/Faravahar" title="Faravahar">Faravahar</a> which mistakenly is being called as the symbol of <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="sidebar sidebar-collapse nomobile nowraplinks"><tbody><tr><th class="sidebar-title">The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">ancient Near East</a></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relief_on_the_Ishtar_Gate,_Pergamenmuseum_4.jpg"><img alt="Relief on the Ishtar Gate, Pergamenmuseum 4.jpg" data-file-height="3017" data-file-width="3354" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Relief_on_the_Ishtar_Gate%2C_Pergamenmuseum_4.jpg/170px-Relief_on_the_Ishtar_Gate%2C_Pergamenmuseum_4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Relief_on_the_Ishtar_Gate%2C_Pergamenmuseum_4.jpg/255px-Relief_on_the_Ishtar_Gate%2C_Pergamenmuseum_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Relief_on_the_Ishtar_Gate%2C_Pergamenmuseum_4.jpg/340px-Relief_on_the_Ishtar_Gate%2C_Pergamenmuseum_4.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content hlist">

<li><a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content hlist">

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:#eee;text-align:center;">Other topics</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content">

<li><a class="mw-selflink selflink">History of the Middle East</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Middle_Eastern_history" title="Timeline of Middle Eastern history">Timeline</a></li></ul></li>
