</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Ancient_Near_East_topics" title="Template:Ancient Near East topics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Ancient_Near_East_topics" title="Template talk:Ancient Near East topics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Ancient_Near_East_topics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The ancient Near East was the first to practice intensive year-round <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currency</a>-mediated <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a> (as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Barter" title="Barter">barter</a>), gave the rest of the world the first <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing" title="History of writing">writing system</a>, invented the <a href="/wiki/Potter%27s_wheel" title="Potter's wheel">potter's wheel</a> and then the vehicular and mill <a href="/wiki/Wheel" title="Wheel">wheel</a>, created the first <a href="/wiki/Centralized_government" title="Centralized government">centralized governments</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_code" title="Law code">law codes</a>, served as birthplace to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cities_of_the_ancient_Near_East" title="Cities of the ancient Near East">first city-states</a> with their high degree of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Division_of_labor" title="Division of labor">division of labor</a>, as well as laying the foundation for the fields of <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>. However, its empires also introduced rigid <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">social stratification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>, and organized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warfare" title="Warfare">warfare</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Cradle_of_civilization.2C_Sumer_and_Akkad"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cradle_of_civilization,_Sumer_and_Akkad">Cradle of civilization, Sumer and Akkad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Cradle of civilization, Sumer and Akkad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The earliest <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilizations</a> in history were established in the region now known as the Middle East around 3500 BC by the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>), widely regarded as the <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_civilization" title="Cradle of civilization">cradle of civilization</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadians</a>, and later <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a> all flourished in this region.
</p><p>"In the course of the fourth millennium BC, city-states developed in southern Mesopotamia that were dominated by temples whose priests represented the cities' patron deities. The most prominent of the city-states was Sumer, which gave its language to the area, [presumably the <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing" title="History of writing">first written language</a>,] and became the first great civilization of mankind. About 2340 BC, <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon the Great</a> (c. 2360–2305 BC) united the city-states in the south and founded the Akkadian dynasty, the world's first empire."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>During this same time period, Sargon the Great appointed his daughter, <a href="/wiki/Enheduanna" title="Enheduanna">Enheduanna</a>, as High Priestess of Inanna at Ur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Her writings, which established her as the first known author in world history, also helped cement Sargon's position in the region.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt">Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a></div>
<p>Soon after the Sumerian civilization began, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_valley" title="Nile valley">Nile valley</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lower_Egypt" title="Lower Egypt">Lower</a> and <a href="/wiki/Upper_Egypt" title="Upper Egypt">Upper Egypt</a> was unified under the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaohs</a> approximately around 3150 BC. Since then, Ancient Egypt experienced 3 high points of civilization, the so-called "Kingdom" periods:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Old Kingdom of Egypt">Old Kingdom</a> (2686–2181),</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom</a> (2055–1650) and, most notably,</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom</a> (1550–1069).</li></ul>
<p>The history of Ancient Egypt is concluded by the <a href="/wiki/Late_Period_of_ancient_Egypt" title="Late Period of ancient Egypt">Late Period</a> (664–332 BC), immediately followed by the history of Egypt in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Antiquity" title="Classical Antiquity">Classical Antiquity</a>, beginning with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Egypt" title="Ptolemaic Egypt">Ptolemaic Egypt</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Levant_and_Anatolia">The Levant and Anatolia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Levant and Anatolia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_ancient_Levant" title="History of the ancient Levant">History of the ancient Levant</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Anatolia" title="History of Anatolia">History of Anatolia</a></div>
<p>Thereafter, civilization quickly spread through the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a> to the east coast of the Mediterranean Sea and throughout the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, as well as to <a href="/wiki/History_of_Anatolia" title="History of Anatolia">ancient Anatolia</a>. Ancient Levantine kingdoms and city states included <a href="/wiki/Ebla" title="Ebla">Ebla</a> City, <a href="/wiki/Ugarit" title="Ugarit">Ugarit</a> City, <a href="/wiki/Aram-Damascus" title="Aram-Damascus">Kingdom of Aram-Damascus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">Kingdom of Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">Kingdom of Judah</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ammon" title="Kingdom of Ammon">Kingdom of Ammon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Moab" title="Kingdom of Moab">Kingdom of Moab</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Edom" title="Kingdom of Edom">Kingdom of Edom</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nabatean_kingdom" title="Nabatean kingdom">Nabatean kingdom</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> civilization, encompassing several city states, was a <a href="/wiki/Thalassocracy" title="Thalassocracy">maritime trading culture</a> that established <a href="/wiki/Colony" title="Colony">colonial</a> cities in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a>, most notably <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Carthage" title="Ancient Carthage">Carthage, in 814 BC</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assyrian_empires">Assyrian empires</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Assyrian empires">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> was home to several powerful empires that came to rule almost the entire Middle East—particularly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Empire" title="Assyrian Empire">Assyrian Empires</a> of 1365–1076 BC and the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> of 911–605 BC. The Assyrian Empire, at its peak, was the largest the world had seen. It ruled all of what is now Iraq, Syria, Lebanon, Israel, Palestine, Kuwait, Jordan, Egypt, Cyprus, and Bahrain—with large swathes of Iran, Turkey, Armenia, Georgia, Sudan, and Arabia.
"The Assyrian empires, particularly the third, had a profound and lasting impact on the Near East. Before Assyrian hegemony ended, the Assyrians brought the highest civilization to the then known world. From the Caspian to Cyprus, from Anatolia to Egypt, Assyrian imperial expansion would bring into the Assyrian sphere nomadic and barbaric communities, and would bestow the gift of civilization upon them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-Babylonian_and_Persian_empires">Neo-Babylonian and Persian empires</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Neo-Babylonian and Persian empires">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Empire" title="Persian Empire">Persian Empire</a></div>
<p>From the early 6th century BC onwards, several Persian states dominated the region, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> and non-Persian <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonian Empire</a>, then their successor the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> known as the first Persian Empire, conquered in the late 4th century BC by the very short-lived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_Empire" title="Macedonian Empire">Macedonian Empire</a> of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, and then successor kingdoms such as <a href="/wiki/History_of_Egypt" title="History of Egypt">Ptolemaic Egypt</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucid" title="Seleucid">Seleucid</a> state in Western Asia.
</p><p>After a century of hiatus, the idea of the Persian Empire was revived by the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> in the 3rd century BC—and continued by their successors, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanids</a> from the 3rd century AD. This empire dominated sizable parts of what is now the Asian part of the Middle East and continued to influence the rest of the Asiatic and African Middle East region, until the Arab <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">Muslim conquest of Persia</a> in the mid-7th century AD. Between the 1st century BC and the early 7th century AD, the region was completely dominated by the Romans and the Parthians and Sassanids on the other hand, which often culminated in various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman-Persian_Wars" title="Roman-Persian Wars">Roman-Persian Wars</a> over the seven centuries. Eastern Rite, <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> Christianity took hold in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asorestan" title="Asorestan">Persian-ruled Mesopotamia</a>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a> from the 1st century AD onwards, and the region became a center of a flourishing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syriac_Christians" title="Syriac Christians">Syriac</a>–<a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian</a> literary tradition.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_and_Roman_Empire">Greek and Roman Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Greek and Roman Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_Empire" title="Macedonian Empire">Macedonian Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RomanEmpire_117.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="376" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/RomanEmpire_117.svg/240px-RomanEmpire_117.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/RomanEmpire_117.svg/360px-RomanEmpire_117.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/RomanEmpire_117.svg/480px-RomanEmpire_117.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RomanEmpire_117.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Roman Empire at its greatest extent, under <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a>, 117 AD</div></div></div>
<p>In 66–63 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman</a> general <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> conquered much of the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> united the region with most of Europe and North Africa in a single political and economic unit. Even areas not directly annexed were strongly influenced by the Empire, which was the most powerful political and cultural entity for centuries. Though <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_ancient_Rome" title="Culture of ancient Rome">Roman culture</a> spread across the region, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_culture" title="Greek culture">Greek culture</a> and language first established in the region by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_Empire" title="Macedonian Empire">Macedonian Empire</a> continued to dominate throughout the Roman period. Cities in the Middle East, especially <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, became major urban centers for the Empire and the region became the Empire's "bread basket" as the key agricultural producer. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egypt_(Roman_province)" title="Egypt (Roman province)">Ægyptus</a> was by far the most wealthy Roman province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jerusalem-Grabeskirche-14-vom_Erloeserkirchturm-2010-gje.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Jerusalem-Grabeskirche-14-vom_Erloeserkirchturm-2010-gje.jpg/240px-Jerusalem-Grabeskirche-14-vom_Erloeserkirchturm-2010-gje.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Jerusalem-Grabeskirche-14-vom_Erloeserkirchturm-2010-gje.jpg/360px-Jerusalem-Grabeskirche-14-vom_Erloeserkirchturm-2010-gje.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Jerusalem-Grabeskirche-14-vom_Erloeserkirchturm-2010-gje.jpg/480px-Jerusalem-Grabeskirche-14-vom_Erloeserkirchturm-2010-gje.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jerusalem-Grabeskirche-14-vom_Erloeserkirchturm-2010-gje.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">Church of the Holy Sepulchre</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>: Jerusalem is generally considered the cradle of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>As the Christian religion spread throughout the Roman and Persian Empires, it took root in the Middle East, and cities such as <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Edessa</a> became important centers of Christian scholarship. By the 5th century, Christianity was the dominant religion in the Middle East, with other faiths (gradually including <i><a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heretical</a></i> Christian sects) being actively repressed. The Middle East's ties to the city of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a> were gradually severed as the Empire <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire#Partition_of_the_Empire" title="Roman Empire">split</a> into <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">West</a>, with the Middle East tied to the new Roman capital of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>. The subsequent <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">Fall of the Western Roman Empire</a> therefore, had minimal direct impact on the region.
</p>
<h4><span id="Byzantine_Empire_.28Eastern_Roman_Empire.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantine_Empire_(Eastern_Roman_Empire)">Byzantine Empire (Eastern Roman Empire)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Byzantine Empire (Eastern Roman Empire)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a></div>
<p>The Eastern Roman Empire, today commonly known as the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, ruling from the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>, became increasingly defined by and dogmatic about Christianity, gradually creating religious rifts between the doctrines dictated by the establishment in Constantinople and believers in many parts of the Middle East. By this time, Greek had become the 'lingua franca' of the region, although ethnicities such as the Syriacs and the Hebrew continued to exist. Under Byzantine/Greek rule the area of the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> met an era of stability and prosperity.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Middle_East">Medieval Middle East<span class="anchor" id="Medieval_Near_East"></span><span class="anchor" id="Medieval_Middle_East"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Medieval Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_history" title="Post-classical history">Post-classical history</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Islam">Pre-Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Pre-Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 5th century, the Middle East was separated into small, weak states; the two most prominent were the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iran" title="History of Iran">Persians</a> in what is now <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, and the Byzantine Empire in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. The Byzantines and Sasanians fought with each other a reflection of the rivalry between the Roman Empire and the Persian Empire seen during the previous five hundred years. The Byzantine-Sasanian rivalry was also seen through their respective cultures and religions. The Byzantines considered themselves champions of <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenism</a> and Christianity. Meanwhile, the Sasanians thought themselves heroes of ancient Iranian traditions and of the traditional Persian religion, <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman-Persian_Frontier,_5th_century.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="769" data-file-width="857" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Roman-Persian_Frontier%2C_5th_century.png/220px-Roman-Persian_Frontier%2C_5th_century.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Roman-Persian_Frontier%2C_5th_century.png/330px-Roman-Persian_Frontier%2C_5th_century.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Roman-Persian_Frontier%2C_5th_century.png/440px-Roman-Persian_Frontier%2C_5th_century.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman-Persian_Frontier,_5th_century.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Roman–Persian frontier after the division of Armenia in 384. The frontier remained stable throughout the 5th century.</div></div></div>
<p>The Arabian peninsula already played a role in the power struggles of the Byzantines and Sasanians. While Byzantium allied itself with the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aksum" title="Kingdom of Aksum">Kingdom of Aksum</a> in the horn of Africa, the Sasanian Empire assisted the <a href="/wiki/Himyarite_Kingdom" title="Himyarite Kingdom">Himyarite Kingdom</a> in what is now <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> (southwest Arabia). Thus the clash between the kingdoms of Aksum and Himyar in 525 displayed a higher power struggle between Byzantium and Persia for control of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> trade. Territorial wars soon became common, with the Byzantines and Sasanians fighting over upper <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and key cities that facilitated trade from Arabia, India, and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Byzantium, as the continuation of the Eastern Roman Empire, continued control of the latter's territories in the Middle East. Since 527, this included Anatolia, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. But in 603 the Sasanians invaded, conquering Damascus and Egypt. It was Emperor <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a> who was able to repel these invasions, and in 628 he replaced the Sasanian Great King with a more docile one. But the fighting weakened both states, leaving the stage open to a new power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWawro2008112–115_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWawro2008112–115-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The nomadic <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> tribes dominated the Arabian desert, where they worshiped <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idols</a> and remained in small clans tied together by kinship. Urbanization and agriculture was limited in Arabia, save for a few regions near the coast. <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> (then called Yathrib) were two such cities that were important hubs for trade between Africa and Eurasia. This commerce was central to city-life, where most inhabitants were merchants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStearnsAdasSchwartzGilbert2011138_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStearnsAdasSchwartzGilbert2011138-29">[29]</a></sup> Nevertheless, some Arabs saw it fit to migrate to the northern regions of the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>, a region so named for its place between the <a href="/wiki/Tigris%E2%80%93Euphrates_river_system" title="Tigris–Euphrates river system">Tigris and Euphrates rivers</a> that offered it fertile land. This included entire tribal chiefdoms such as the <a href="/wiki/Lakhmids" title="Lakhmids">Lakhmids</a> in a less controlled area of the Sasanian Empire, and the <a href="/wiki/Ghassanids" title="Ghassanids">Ghassanids</a> in a similar area inside of Byzantine territory; these political units of Arab origin offered a surprising stability that was rare in the region and offered Arabia further connections to the outside world. The Lakhmid capital, <a href="/wiki/Al-Hirah" title="Al-Hirah">Hira</a> was a center for Christianity and Jewish craftsmen, merchants, and farmers were common in western Arabia as were Christian monks in central Arabia. Thus pre-Islamic Arabia was no stranger to Abrahamic religions or monotheism, for that matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHourani2013The_world_into_which_the_Arabs_came_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHourani2013The_world_into_which_the_Arabs_came-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_caliphate">Islamic caliphate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Islamic caliphate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Intermezzo" title="Iranian Intermezzo">Iranian Intermezzo</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/350px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/525px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/700px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Age of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">Caliphs</a> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#a1584e; color:white;"> </span> Expansion under <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, 622–632</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ef9070; color:black;"> </span> Expansion during the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>, 632–661</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#fad07d; color:black;"> </span> Expansion during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad" title="Umayyad">Umayyad</a> <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a>, 661–750</div></div></div></div>
<p>While the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> Roman and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanian_Empire" title="Sassanian Empire">Sassanid</a> Persian empires were both weakened by <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_War_of_602%E2%80%93628" title="Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628">warfare (602–628)</a>, a new power in the form of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> grew in the Middle East. In a series of rapid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_army" title="Rashidun army">Arab armies</a>, led by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">Caliphs</a> and skilled military commanders such as <a href="/wiki/Khalid_ibn_al-Walid" title="Khalid ibn al-Walid">Khalid ibn al-Walid</a>, swept through most of the Middle East, taking more than half of <a href="/wiki/Arab%E2%80%93Byzantine_wars" title="Arab–Byzantine wars">Byzantine territory</a> and completely <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">engulfing the Persian lands</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, they were stopped in the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(717%E2%80%93718)" title="Siege of Constantinople (717–718)">Siege of Constantinople (717–718)</a> by the Byzantines, who were helped by the <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">Bulgarians</a>.
</p><p>The Byzantine provinces of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Syria" title="Muslim conquest of Syria">Roman Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_the_Maghreb" title="Muslim conquest of the Maghreb">North Africa</a>, and Sicily, however, could not mount such a resistance, and the Muslim conquerors swept through those regions. At the far west, they crossed the sea taking <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">Visigothic Hispania</a> before being halted in southern France in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tours" title="Battle of Tours">Battle of Tours</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a>. At its greatest extent, the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Arab Empire</a> was the first empire to control the entire Middle East, as well three-quarters of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean region</a>, the only other empire besides the Roman Empire to control most of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> It would be the Arab <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphates</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> that would first unify the entire Middle East as a distinct region and create the dominant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">ethnic identity</a> that persists today. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuq_Empire" title="Seljuq Empire">Seljuq Empire</a> would also later dominate the region.
</p><p>Much of North Africa became a peripheral area to the main Muslim centres in the Middle East, but Iberia (<a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>) and Morocco soon broke away from this distant control and founded one of the world's most advanced societies at the time, along with <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> in the eastern Mediterranean. Between 831 and 1071, the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Sicily" title="Emirate of Sicily">Emirate of Sicily</a> was one of the major centres of Islamic culture in the Mediterranean. After its conquest by the <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a> the island developed its own distinct culture with the fusion of Arab, Western, and Byzantine influences. <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a> remained a leading artistic and commercial centre of the Mediterranean well into the Middle Ages.
</p><p>Africa was reviving, however, as more organized and centralized states began to form in the later <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_of_the_12th_century" title="Renaissance of the 12th century">Renaissance of the 12th century</a>. Motivated by religion and conquest, the kings of Europe launched a number of <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> to try to roll back Muslim power and retake the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a>. The Crusades were unsuccessful but were far more effective in weakening the already tottering Byzantine Empire. They also rearranged the <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_power_(international_relations)" title="Balance of power (international relations)">balance of power</a> in the Muslim world as Egypt once again emerged as a major power.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_culture_and_science">Islamic culture and science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Islamic culture and science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>, <a href="/wiki/Early_social_changes_under_Islam" title="Early social changes under Islam">Early social changes under Islam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Science in the medieval Islamic world">Science in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Interior_de_la_mezquita_de_C%C3%B3rdoba.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Interior_de_la_mezquita_de_C%C3%B3rdoba.jpg/220px-Interior_de_la_mezquita_de_C%C3%B3rdoba.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Interior_de_la_mezquita_de_C%C3%B3rdoba.jpg/330px-Interior_de_la_mezquita_de_C%C3%B3rdoba.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Interior_de_la_mezquita_de_C%C3%B3rdoba.jpg/440px-Interior_de_la_mezquita_de_C%C3%B3rdoba.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Interior_de_la_mezquita_de_C%C3%B3rdoba.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The interior of the former mosque of Córdoba, showing its distinctive arches.</div></div></div>
<p>Religion always played a prevalent role in Middle Eastern culture, affecting learning, architecture, and the ebb and flow of cultures. When Muhammad introduced Islam, it jump-started Middle Eastern culture, inspiring achievements in <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>, the revival of old advances in science and technology, and the formation of a distinct way of life. Islam primarily consisted of the <a href="/wiki/Five_Pillars_of_Islam" title="Five Pillars of Islam">five pillars of belief</a>, including confession of faith, the five prayers a day, to <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fast</a> during the holy month of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a>, to pay the tax for charity (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zak%C4%81t" title="Zakāt">zakāt</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">hajj</a>, or the pilgrimage that a Muslim needed to take at least once in their lifetime, according to the five (or six) pillars of Islam. Islam also created the need for spectacularly built <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosques</a> which created a distinct form of architecture. Some of the more magnificent mosques include the <a href="/wiki/Temple_Mount" title="Temple Mount">Al-Aqsa Mosque</a> and the former <a href="/wiki/Mosque%E2%80%93Cathedral_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Mosque–Cathedral of Córdoba">Mosque of Cordoba</a>. Islam unified the Middle East and helped the empires there to remain stable. Missionaries and warriors spread the religion from Arabia to North and Sudanic Africa, South and Southeast Asia, and the Mesopotamia area. This created a mix of cultures, especially in Africa, and the mawali demographic. Although the mawali would experience discrimination from the Umayyad, they would gain widespread acceptance from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid" title="Abbasid">Abbasids</a> and it was because of this that allowed for mass conversions in foreign areas. "People of the book" or dhimmi were always treated well; these people included Christians, Jews, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrians</a>. However, the crusades started a new thinking in the Islamic empires, that non-Islamic ideas were immoral or inferior; this was primarily perpetrated by the ulama (علماء) scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStearnsAdasSchwartzGilbert2011171_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStearnsAdasSchwartzGilbert2011171-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arabian culture took off during the early Abbasid age, despite the prevalent political issues. Muslims saved and spread Greek advances in <a href="/wiki/History_of_medicine" title="History of medicine">medicine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_elementary_algebra" title="History of elementary algebra">algebra</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_geometry" title="History of geometry">geometry</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_astronomy" title="History of astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_anatomy" title="History of anatomy">anatomy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_ethics" title="History of ethics">ethics</a> that would later find its way back to Western Europe. The works of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> were saved and distributed throughout the empire (and eventually into Europe) in this manner. Muslim scholars also discovered the <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="Hindu–Arabic numeral system">Hindu–Arabic numeral system</a> in their <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="Muslim conquests in the Indian subcontinent">conquests of south Asia</a>. The use of this system in Muslim trade and political institutions allowed for the eventual popularization of it around the world; this number system would be critical to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" title="Scientific revolution">Scientific revolution</a> in Europe. Muslim intellectuals would become experts in <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mapmaking" title="Mapmaking">mapmaking</a> during the Abbasid Caliphate. In the arts, <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_architecture" title="Abbasid architecture">Abbasid architecture</a> expanded upon <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_architecture" title="Umayyad architecture">Umayyad architecture</a>, with larger and more extravagant mosques. <a href="/wiki/Persian_literature" title="Persian literature">Persian literature</a> grew based on ethical values. Astronomy was stressed in art. Much of this learning would find its way to the West. This was especially true during the crusades, as warriors would bring back Muslim treasures, weapons, and medicinal methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStearnsAdasSchwartzGilbert2011159_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStearnsAdasSchwartzGilbert2011159-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Turks.2C_Crusaders.2C_and_Mongols"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Turks,_Crusaders,_and_Mongols">Turks, Crusaders, and Mongols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Turks, Crusaders, and Mongols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_conquests" title="Mongol conquests">Mongol conquests</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Jerusalem" title="History of Jerusalem">History of Jerusalem</a></div>
<p>The dominance of the Arabs came to a sudden end in the mid-11th century with the arrival of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuq_Turks" title="Seljuq Turks">Seljuq Turks</a>, migrating south from the Turkic homelands in Central Asia. They conquered Persia, Iraq (capturing Baghdad in 1055), Syria, Palestine, and the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a>. Egypt held out under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid_caliph" title="Fatimid caliph">Fatimid caliphs</a> until 1169, when it too fell to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayyubid_Dynasty" title="Ayyubid Dynasty">Turks</a>.
</p><p>Despite massive territorial losses in the 7th century, the Christian <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> continued to be a potent military and economic force in the Mediterranean, preventing Arab expansion into much of Europe. The Seljuqs' defeat of the Byzantine military in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manzikert" title="Battle of Manzikert">Battle of Manzikert</a> in the 11th century and settling in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> effectively marked the end of Byzantine power. The Seljuks ruled most of the Middle East region for the next 200 years, but their empire soon broke up into a number of smaller sultanates.
</p><p>Christian Western Europe staged a remarkable economic and demographic recovery in the 11th century since its nadir in the 7th century. The fragmentation of the Middle East allowed joined forces, mainly from England, France, and the emerging <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, to enter the region. In 1095, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_II" title="Pope Urban II">Pope Urban II</a> responded to pleas from the flagging Byzantine Empire and summoned the European aristocracy to recapture the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> for Christianity. In 1099 the knights of the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a> captured <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> and founded the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">Kingdom of Jerusalem</a>, which survived until 1187, when <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">Saladin</a>, the founder of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayyubid" title="Ayyubid">Ayyubid</a> dynasty, retook the city. Smaller crusader kingdoms and fiefdoms survived until 1291.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mongol_rule">Mongol rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Mongol rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_and_conquests#West_Asia" title="Mongol invasions and conquests">Mongol invasions and conquests § West Asia</a></div>
<p>The conquest of Baghdad and the death of the caliph in 1258 officiated the end of the Abbasid Caliphate and annexed its territories to the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>, excluding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamluk_Egypt" title="Mamluk Egypt">Mamluk Egypt</a> and the majority of Arabia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWawro2008146–149_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWawro2008146–149-34">[34]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Khagan" title="Khagan">Khagan</a> (or Great Khan) of the Mongol Empire, <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ngke_Khan" title="Möngke Khan">Möngke Khan</a>, died in 1259, any further expansion by Hulegu was halted, as he had to return to the Mongol capital <a href="/wiki/Karakorum" title="Karakorum">Karakorum</a> for the election of a new khagan. His absence resulted in the first defeat of the Mongols (by the Mamluk Egyptians) during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ain_Jalut" title="Battle of Ain Jalut">Battle of Ain Jalut</a> in 1260.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuzman1985230–233_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuzman1985230–233-35">[35]</a></sup> Issues began to arise when the Mongols grew increasingly unable to reach a consensus as to whom to elect khagan. Additionally, societal clashing occurred between traditionalists who wished to retain their nomadic culture and Mongols moving towards sedentary agriculture. All of this led to the fragmentation of the empire in 1260.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rossabi_Mongol_conquests_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rossabi_Mongol_conquests-36">[36]</a></sup> Hulegu carved out his Middle Eastern territory into the independent <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a>, which included most of Armenia, Anatolia, Azerbaijan, Mesopotamia, and Iran.
</p><p>The Mongols eventually retreated in 1335, but the chaos that ensued throughout the empire deposed the Seljuq Turks. In 1401, the region was further plagued by the Turko-Mongol, <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a>, and his ferocious raids. By then, another group of Turks had arisen as well, the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">Ottomans</a>. Based in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, by 1566 they would conquer the Iraq-Iran region, the Balkans, Greece, Byzantium, most of Egypt, most of north Africa, and parts of Arabia, unifying them under the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. The rule of the Ottoman sultans marked the end of the Medieval (Postclassical) Era in the Middle East.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Modern_Near_East">Early Modern Near East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Early Modern Near East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_modern_history" title="Early modern history">Early modern history</a></div>
<h3><span id="The_Ottoman_Empire_.281299.E2.80.931918.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Ottoman_Empire_(1299–1918)">The Ottoman Empire (1299–1918)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: The Ottoman Empire (1299–1918)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output
