<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OttomanEmpire1590.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2581" data-file-width="3596" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/OttomanEmpire1590.png/220px-OttomanEmpire1590.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/OttomanEmpire1590.png/330px-OttomanEmpire1590.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/OttomanEmpire1590.png/440px-OttomanEmpire1590.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OttomanEmpire1590.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Ottoman Empire at its greatest extent in the Middle East, including <a href="/wiki/Vassal_and_tributary_states_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Vassal and tributary states of the Ottoman Empire">its client states</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:I_Selim.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="690" data-file-width="548" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/I_Selim.jpg/220px-I_Selim.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/I_Selim.jpg/330px-I_Selim.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/I_Selim.jpg/440px-I_Selim.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:I_Selim.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selim_the_Grim" title="Selim the Grim">Selim the Grim</a>, Ottoman conqueror of the Middle East</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:C)_Armenian_Girl,_Druse,_Inhabitant_of_Damascus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="404" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/C%29_Armenian_Girl%2C_Druse%2C_Inhabitant_of_Damascus.jpg/220px-C%29_Armenian_Girl%2C_Druse%2C_Inhabitant_of_Damascus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/C%29_Armenian_Girl%2C_Druse%2C_Inhabitant_of_Damascus.jpg/330px-C%29_Armenian_Girl%2C_Druse%2C_Inhabitant_of_Damascus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/C%29_Armenian_Girl%2C_Druse%2C_Inhabitant_of_Damascus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:C)_Armenian_Girl,_Druse,_Inhabitant_of_Damascus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inhabitants of Damascus by the end of the Ottoman era</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Ottoman_empire_in_1916_-_Banse_Ewald_-_1919.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1362" data-file-width="1792" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Map_of_the_Ottoman_empire_in_1916_-_Banse_Ewald_-_1919.jpg/300px-Map_of_the_Ottoman_empire_in_1916_-_Banse_Ewald_-_1919.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Map_of_the_Ottoman_empire_in_1916_-_Banse_Ewald_-_1919.jpg/450px-Map_of_the_Ottoman_empire_in_1916_-_Banse_Ewald_-_1919.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Map_of_the_Ottoman_empire_in_1916_-_Banse_Ewald_-_1919.jpg/600px-Map_of_the_Ottoman_empire_in_1916_-_Banse_Ewald_-_1919.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Ottoman_empire_in_1916_-_Banse_Ewald_-_1919.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Middle East Map, 1916</div></div></div>
<p>By the early 15th century, a new power had arisen in western Anatolia, the Ottoman Empire. Ottoman khans, who in 1453 captured the Christian Byzantine capitol of Constantinople and made themselves sultans. The Mamluks held the Ottomans out of the Middle East for a century, but in 1514 <a href="/wiki/Selim_I" title="Selim I">Selim the Grim</a> began the systematic Ottoman conquest of the region. Syria was occupied in 1516 and Egypt in 1517, extinguishing the Mameluk line. Iraq was conquered almost in 40 years from the Iranian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safavids" title="Safavids">Safavids</a>, who were successors of the <a href="/wiki/Aq_Qoyunlu" title="Aq Qoyunlu">Aq Qoyunlu</a>.
</p><p>The Ottomans united the whole region under one ruler for the first time since the reign of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid_caliphs" title="Abbasid caliphs">Abbasid caliphs</a> of the 10th century, and they kept control of it for 400 years, despite brief intermissions created by the Iranian Safavids and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afsharids" title="Afsharids">Afsharids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000-37">[37]</a></sup> By this time the Ottomans also held <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, and most of <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, setting the new frontier between east and west far to the north of the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>.
</p><p>In the west, Europe was rapidly expanding, demographically, economically, and culturally. By 1700, the Ottomans had been driven out of Hungary. Although some areas of Ottoman Europe, such as <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_Eyalet" title="Bosnia Eyalet">Bosnia</a>, saw many conversions to Islam, the area was never culturally absorbed into the Muslim world. From 1768 to 1918, the Ottomans gradually lost territory. By the 19th century, Europe had overtaken the Muslim world in wealth, population, and—most importantly—technology. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> fueled a boom that laid the foundations for the growth of <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>. During the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> claimed independence, and the Ottoman Empire became known as the "<a href="/wiki/Sick_man_of_Europe" title="Sick man of Europe">sick man of Europe</a>", increasingly under the financial control of European powers. Domination soon turned to outright conquest: the French annexed <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> in 1830 and <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> in 1878 and the British occupied Egypt in 1882, though it remained under nominal Ottoman sovereignty. In the <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Wars" title="Balkan Wars">Balkan Wars</a> of 1912–13 the Ottomans were driven out of Europe altogether, except for the city of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and its hinterland.
</p><p>The British also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Residency_of_the_Persian_Gulf" title="British Residency of the Persian Gulf">established effective control</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, and the French extended their influence into <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> and Syria. In 1912, the Italians seized <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dodecanese" title="Dodecanese">Dodecanese islands</a>, just off the coast of the Ottoman heartland of Anatolia. The Ottomans turned to Germany to protect them from the western powers, but the result was increasing financial and military dependence on Germany.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_reform_efforts">Ottoman reform efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Ottoman reform efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the late 19th and early 20th centuries, Middle Eastern rulers tried to modernize their states to compete more effectively with Europe. In the Ottoman Empire, the <a href="/wiki/Tanzimat" title="Tanzimat">Tanzimat</a> reforms re-invigorated Ottoman rule and were furthered by the <a href="/wiki/Young_Ottomans" title="Young Ottomans">Young Ottomans</a> in the late 19th century, leading to the <a href="/wiki/First_Constitutional_Era" title="First Constitutional Era">First Constitutional Era</a> in the Empire that included the writing of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1876_Ottoman_constitution" title="1876 Ottoman constitution">1876 constitution</a> and the establishment of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Parliament" title="Ottoman Parliament">Ottoman Parliament</a>. The authors of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Constitutional_Revolution" title="Persian Constitutional Revolution">1906 revolution in Persia</a> all sought to import versions of the western model of constitutional government, civil law, secular education, and industrial development into their countries. Throughout the region, railways and telegraph lines were constructed, schools and universities were opened, and a new class of army officers, lawyers, teachers, and administrators emerged, challenging the traditional leadership of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_scholars" title="Islamic scholars">Islamic scholars</a>.
</p><p>This first Ottoman constitutional experiment ended soon after it began, however, when the autocratic Sultan <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Hamid_II" title="Abdul Hamid II">Abdul Hamid II</a> abolished the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Parliament" title="Ottoman Parliament">parliament</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1876_Ottoman_constitution" title="1876 Ottoman constitution">constitution</a> in favor of personal rule. Abdul Hamid ruled by decree for the next 30 years, stirring democratic resentment. The reform movement known as the <a href="/wiki/Young_Turks" title="Young Turks">Young Turks</a> emerged in the 1890s against his rule, which included <a href="/wiki/Hamidian_massacres" title="Hamidian massacres">massacres against minorities</a>. The Young Turks seized power in the 1908 <a href="/wiki/Young_Turk_Revolution" title="Young Turk Revolution">Young Turk Revolution</a> and established the <a href="/wiki/Second_Constitutional_Era" title="Second Constitutional Era">Second Constitutional Era</a>, leading to a pluralist and multiparty elections in the Empire for the first time <a href="/wiki/1908_Ottoman_general_election" title="1908 Ottoman general election">in 1908</a>. The Young Turks split into two parties, the pro-German and pro-centralization <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Union_and_Progress" title="Committee of Union and Progress">Committee of Union and Progress</a> and the pro-British and pro-decentralization <a href="/wiki/Freedom_and_Accord_Party" title="Freedom and Accord Party">Freedom and Accord Party</a>. The former was led by an ambitious pair of army officers, <a href="/wiki/Enver_Pasha" title="Enver Pasha">Ismail Enver Bey</a> (later Pasha) and <a href="/wiki/Djemal_Pasha" title="Djemal Pasha">Ahmed Cemal Pasha</a>, and a radical lawyer, <a href="/wiki/Talaat_Pasha" title="Talaat Pasha">Mehmed Talaat Bey (later Pasha)</a>. After a power struggle between the two parties of Young Turks, the Committee <a href="/wiki/1913_Ottoman_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1913 Ottoman coup d'état">emerged victorious</a> and became a ruling junta, with Talaat as Grand Vizier and Enver as War Minister, and established a German-funded modernisation program across the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMansfieldPelham2013141–147_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMansfieldPelham2013141–147-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Enver Bey's alliance with Germany, which he considered the most advanced military power in Europe, was enabled by British demands that the Ottoman Empire cede their formal capital <a href="/wiki/Edirne" title="Edirne">Edirne</a> (Adrianople) to the Bulgarians after losing the <a href="/wiki/First_Balkan_War" title="First Balkan War">First Balkan War</a>, which the Turks saw as a betrayal by Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zürcher_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zürcher-39">[39]</a></sup> These demands cost Britain the support of the Turks, as the pro-British Freedom and Accord Party was now repressed under the pro-German Committee for, in Enver's words, "shamefully delivering the country to the enemy" (Britain) after agreeing to the demands to give up Edirne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L'Illustration_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-L'Illustration-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Middle_East">Modern Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Modern Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_history" title="Modern history">Modern history</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_of_the_Ottoman_Empire">Final years of the Ottoman Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Final years of the Ottoman Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partitioning_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Partitioning of the Ottoman Empire">Partitioning of the Ottoman Empire</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon" title="French Mandate for Syria and the Lebanon">French Mandate for Syria and the Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">Mandatory Palestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Iraq" title="Mandatory Iraq">Mandatory Iraq</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_history_of_Cyprus" title="Modern history of Cyprus">Modern history of Cyprus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protectorate_of_Cyprus" title="Protectorate of Cyprus">Protectorate of Cyprus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_modern_conflicts_in_the_Middle_East" title="List of modern conflicts in the Middle East">List of modern conflicts in the Middle East</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mustafa_Kemal_November_1918.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="283" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Mustafa_Kemal_November_1918.png/180px-Mustafa_Kemal_November_1918.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Mustafa_Kemal_November_1918.png/270px-Mustafa_Kemal_November_1918.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/50/Mustafa_Kemal_November_1918.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mustafa_Kemal_November_1918.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal Atatürk">Mustafa Kemal Atatürk</a>, Ottoman general and the founder of modern Turkey</div></div></div>
<p>In 1878, as the result of the <a href="/wiki/Cyprus_Convention" title="Cyprus Convention">Cyprus Convention</a>, the United Kingdom took over the government of <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> as a protectorate from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. While the Cypriots at first welcomed <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British rule</a>, hoping that they would gradually achieve prosperity, democracy and national liberation, they soon became disillusioned. The British imposed heavy taxes to cover the compensation they paid to the Sultan for conceding Cyprus to them. Moreover, the people were not given the right to participate in the administration of the island, since all powers were reserved to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Commissioner" title="High Commissioner">High Commissioner</a> and to London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, the fall of the Ottomans and the partitioning of Anatolia by the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allies</a> led to resistance by the Turkish population, under the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_National_Movement" title="Turkish National Movement">Turkish National Movement</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal Atatürk">Mustafa Kemal Atatürk</a>, the Turkish victory against the invading powers during the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_War_of_Independence" title="Turkish War of Independence">Turkish War of Independence</a>, and the founding of the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Turkey" title="Republic of Turkey">Republic of Turkey</a> in 1923. As the first President of Turkey, Atatürk embarked on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atat%C3%BCrk%27s_Reforms" title="Atatürk's Reforms">program of modernisation and secularisation</a>. He abolished the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">caliphate</a>, emancipated women, enforced western dress and the use of a new <a href="/wiki/Turkish_alphabet" title="Turkish alphabet">Turkish alphabet</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> in place of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_alphabet" title="Ottoman Turkish alphabet">Arabic alphabet</a>, and abolished the jurisdiction of the Islamic courts. In effect, Turkey, having given up rule over the Arab world, was now determined to secede from the Middle East and become culturally part of Europe.
</p><p>Another turning point came when <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> was discovered, first in Persia (1908) and later in <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> (1938) as well as the other Persian Gulf states, <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>. The Middle East, it turned out, possessed the world's largest easily untapped reserves of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a>, the most important commodity in the 20th century. The kings and emirs of these oil states became immensely wealthy exporting petroleum to the west, allowing them to consolidate their hold on power and giving them a stake in preserving western hegemony over the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morton_39–54_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_39–54-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>A Western dependence on Middle Eastern oil and the decline of British influence led to a growing American interest in the region. Initially, the Western oil companies established a dominance over oil production and extraction. However, indigenous movements towards <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalizing</a> oil assets, oil sharing, and the advent of <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> ensured a shift in the <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_power_(international_relations)" title="Balance of power (international relations)">balance of power</a> towards the Arab oil states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morton_39–54_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_39–54-42">[42]</a></sup> Oil wealth also had the effect of suffocating whatever economic, political, or social reform might have emerged in the Arab world under the influence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atat%C3%BCrk%27s_Reforms" title="Atatürk's Reforms">Kemalist revolution</a> in Turkey.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: World War I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1914, <a href="/wiki/Enver_Pasha" title="Enver Pasha">Enver Pasha</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93German_Alliance" title="Ottoman–German Alliance">alliance with Germany</a> led the Ottoman Empire into the fatal step of joining Germany and <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, against Britain and France. The British saw the Ottomans as the weak link in the enemy alliance, and concentrated on knocking them out of the war. When a direct assault failed at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallipoli_Campaign" title="Gallipoli Campaign">Gallipoli</a> in 1915, they turned to fomenting revolution in the Ottoman domains, exploiting the awakening force of <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">Arab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_nationalism" title="Armenian nationalism">Armenian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_nationalism" title="Assyrian nationalism">Assyrian nationalism</a> against the Ottomans.
</p><p>The British found an ally in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hussein_bin_Ali,_Sharif_of_Mecca" title="Hussein bin Ali, Sharif of Mecca">Sharif Hussein</a>, the hereditary ruler of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> (and believed by Muslims to be a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>), who led an <a href="/wiki/Arab_Revolt" title="Arab Revolt">Arab Revolt</a> against Ottoman rule, after being promised independence.
</p><p>The Allies, led by Britain, won the war and seized most of the Ottoman territories; Turkey just managed to survive. The war transformed the region in terms of increased British and French involvement; the creation of the Middle Eastern state system as seen in Turkey and Saudi Arabia; the emergence of explicitly more nationalist politics, as seen in Turkey and Egypt; and the rapid growth of the Middle Eastern oil industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Ottoman_defeat_and_partition_.281918.E2.80.9322.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_defeat_and_partition_(1918–22)">Ottoman defeat and partition (1918–22)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Ottoman defeat and partition (1918–22)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Partition of the Ottoman Empire">Partition of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>
<p>When the Ottoman Empire was defeated by an Arab uprising and the British forces after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinai_and_Palestine_Campaign" title="Sinai and Palestine Campaign">Sinai and Palestine Campaign</a> in 1918, the Arab population did not get what it wanted. Islamic activists of more recent times have described it as an Anglo-French betrayal. British and French governments concluded a secret treaty (the <a href="/wiki/Sykes%E2%80%93Picot_Agreement" title="Sykes–Picot Agreement">Sykes–Picot Agreement</a>) to partition the Middle East between them. The British in 1917 announced the <a href="/wiki/Balfour_Declaration" title="Balfour Declaration">Balfour Declaration</a> promised the international <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">Zionist</a> movement their support in re-creating the historic Jewish homeland in Palestine.
</p><p>When the Ottomans departed, the Arabs proclaimed an independent state in <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, but were too weak, militarily and economically, to resist the European powers for long, and Britain and France soon established control and re-arranged the Middle East to suit themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> became a French protectorate as a <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">League of Nations mandate</a>. The Christian coastal areas were split off to become <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, another French protectorate. <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> became British mandated territories. Iraq became the "<a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq" title="Kingdom of Iraq">Kingdom of Iraq</a>" and one of Sharif Hussein's sons, <a href="/wiki/Faisal_I_of_Iraq" title="Faisal I of Iraq">Faisal</a>, was installed as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Iraq" title="King of Iraq">King of Iraq</a>. Iraq incorporated large populations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" title="Kurdish people">Kurds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkmen_people" title="Turkmen people">Turkmens</a>, many of whom had been promised independent states of their own.
</p><p>Britain was granted a Mandate for Palestine on 25 April 1920 at the San Remo Conference, and, on 24 July 1922, this mandate was approved by the League of Nations. Palestine became the "<a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">British Mandate of Palestine</a>" and  was placed under direct British administration. The Jewish population of Palestine which numbered less than 8 percent in 1918 was given free rein to immigrate, buy land from absentee landlords, set up a shadow government in waiting and establish the nucleus of a state under the protection of the British Army which suppressed a Palestinian revolt in 1936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The Territory East of the Jordan River was added to the British Mandate by the Transjordan Memorandum, which was a British memorandum passed by the Council of the League of Nations on 16 September 1922. Most of the Arabian peninsula fell to another British ally, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Saud" title="Ibn Saud">Ibn Saud</a>. Saud created the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> in 1932.
</p>
<h3><span id="1920.E2.80.931945"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1920–1945">1920–1945</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: 1920–1945">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the 1920s, 1930s, and 1940s, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> made moves towards independence. In 1919, Egypt's <a href="/wiki/Saad_Zaghloul" title="Saad Zaghloul">Saad Zaghloul</a> orchestrated mass demonstrations in Egypt known as the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Revolution_of_1919" title="Egyptian Revolution of 1919">First Revolution</a>. While Zaghloul would later become Prime Minister, the British repression of the anticolonial riots led to around 800 deaths. In 1920, Syrian forces were defeated by the French in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Maysalun" title="Battle of Maysalun">Battle of Maysalun</a> and Iraqi forces were defeated by the British when they <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iraqi_revolt_against_the_British" title="Iraqi revolt against the British">revolted</a>. In 1922, the (nominally) independent <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Kingdom of Egypt">Kingdom of Egypt</a> was created following the British government's issuance of the <a href="/wiki/Unilateral_Declaration_of_Egyptian_Independence" title="Unilateral Declaration of Egyptian Independence">Unilateral Declaration of Egyptian Independence</a>.
</p><p>Although the Kingdom of Egypt was technically "neutral" during World War II, <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a> soon became a major military base for the British and the country was occupied. The British cited the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_treaty_of_1936" title="Anglo-Egyptian treaty of 1936">1936 treaty</a> that allowed it to station troops on Egyptian soil to protect the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>. In 1941, the <a href="/wiki/1941_Iraqi_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1941 Iraqi coup d'état">Rashīd `Alī al-Gaylānī coup</a> in Iraq led to the British to invade, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Iraqi_War" title="Anglo-Iraqi War">Anglo-Iraqi War</a>. This was followed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syria%E2%80%93Lebanon_Campaign" title="Syria–Lebanon Campaign">Allied invasion of Syria–Lebanon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Soviet_invasion_of_Iran" title="Anglo-Soviet invasion of Iran">Anglo-Soviet invasion of Iran</a>.
</p><p>In Palestine, conflicting forces of <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">Arab nationalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">Zionism</a> created a situation the British could neither resolve nor extricate themselves from. The rise of German dictator <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> had created a new urgency in the Zionist quest to immigrate to Palestine and create a <a href="/wiki/Jewish_state" title="Jewish state">Jewish state</a>. A Palestinian state was also an attractive alternative to the Arab and Persian leaders, instead of the de facto British, French, and perceived Jewish colonialism or imperialism, under the logic of "<a href="/wiki/The_enemy_of_my_enemy_is_my_friend" title="The enemy of my enemy is my friend">the enemy of my enemy is my friend</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis1995348–350_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis1995348–350-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_states_after_World_War_II">New states after World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: New states after World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The British,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> French, and Soviets departed from many parts of the Middle East during and after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. Iran, Turkey, Saudi Arabia, and the states in the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> generally kept their boundaries.  After the war, however, seven Middle East states gained (or regained) their independence:
</p>
<ul><li>22 November 1943 – Lebanon</li>
<li>1 January 1944 – Syria</li>
<li>22 May 1946 – Jordan (British mandate ended)</li>
<li>1947 – Iraq (forces of the United Kingdom withdrawn)</li>
<li>1947 – Egypt (forces of the United Kingdom withdrawn to the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> area)</li>
<li>1948 – Israel (forces of the United Kingdom withdrawn)</li>
<li>August 16, 1960 – Cyprus</li></ul>
<p>The struggle between the Arabs and the Jews in Palestine culminated in the 1947 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1947_UN_Partition_Plan" title="1947 UN Partition Plan">plan to partition Palestine</a>. This plan sought to create an Arab state and a separate Jewish state in the narrow space between the <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River" title="Jordan River">Jordan River</a> and the Mediterranean. The Jewish leaders accepted it, but the Arab leaders rejected this plan.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>On 14 May 1948, when the British Mandate expired, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zionist" title="Zionist">Zionist</a> leadership declared the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Israel" title="State of Israel">State of Israel</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/1948_Arab%E2%80%93Israeli_War" title="1948 Arab–Israeli War">1948 Arab–Israeli War</a> which immediately followed, the armies of Egypt, Syria, Transjordan, Lebanon, Iraq, and Saudi Arabia intervened and were defeated by Israel.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> About 800,000 Palestinians fled from areas annexed by Israel and became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palestinian_refugee" title="Palestinian refugee">refugees</a> in neighbouring countries, thus creating the "Palestinian problem",<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> which has troubled the region ever since. Approximately two-thirds of 758,000–866,000 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_exodus_from_Arab_and_Muslim_countries" title="Jewish exodus from Arab and Muslim countries">Jews expelled or who fled from Arab lands</a> after 1948 were absorbed and naturalized by the State of Israel.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>On August 16, 1960, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> gained its independence from the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">United Kingdom</a>. Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Makarios_III" title="Makarios III">Makarios III</a>, a charismatic religious and political leader, was elected its first independent president, and in 1961 it became the 99th member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_states">Modern states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Modern states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Middle_East" title="Economy of the Middle East">Economy of the Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_modern_conflicts_in_the_Middle_East" title="List of modern conflicts in the Middle East">List of modern conflicts in the Middle East</a></div>

codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="316392" data-framerate="29.97" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e0/The_Middle_East.ogv/The_Middle_East.ogv.240p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="326416" data-framerate="29.97" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e0/The_Middle_East.ogv/The_Middle_East.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Middle_East.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1963 film about contemporary events in the Middle East</div></div></div>
<p>The modern Middle East was shaped by three things: departure of European powers, the <a href="/wiki/Israeli_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Israeli Declaration of Independence">founding of Israel</a>, and the growing importance of the oil industry. These developments eventually led to increased <a href="/wiki/United_States_foreign_policy_in_the_Middle_East" title="United States foreign policy in the Middle East">U.S. involvement in the region</a>. The United States was the ultimate guarantor of the region's stability as well as the dominant force in the oil industry after the 1950s. When revolutions brought radical <a href="/wiki/Anti-Western_sentiment" title="Anti-Western sentiment">anti-Western</a> regimes to power in Egypt (1954), Syria (1963), Iraq (1968), and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> (1969), the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, seeking to open a new arena of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, allied itself with <a href="/wiki/Arab_socialism" title="Arab socialism">Arab socialist</a> rulers like <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a> in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>.
</p><p>These regimes gained popular support with promises to destroy the state of Israel and other "western imperialists", and to bring prosperity to the Arab masses. When the <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">Six-Day War</a> of 1967 ended with an overwhelming Israeli victory, many viewed the defeat as the failure of Arab socialism. This represents a turning point when "<a href="/wiki/Islamic_fundamentalism" title="Islamic fundamentalism">fundamental</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_terrorism" title="Islamic terrorism">militant Islam</a> began to fill the political vacuum created".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watson_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-watson-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United States, in response, felt obliged to defend its remaining allies, the monarchies of Saudi Arabia, Jordan, Iran, and the Persian Gulf emirates, whose methods of rule were often almost as unattractive in western eyes as those of the anti-western regimes. Iran in particular became a key U.S. ally, until a revolution led by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shi%27a" title="Shi'a">Shi'a</a> clergy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_revolution" title="Iranian revolution">overthrew the monarchy in 1979</a> and established a <a href="/wiki/Theocracy" title="Theocracy">theocratic state</a> that proved to be far more anti-western than the secular regimes in Iraq or Syria. The list of Arab-Israeli wars includes a great number of major wars such as <a href="/wiki/1948_Arab%E2%80%93Israeli_War" title="1948 Arab–Israeli War">1948 Arab–Israeli War</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1956_Suez_War" title="1956 Suez War">1956 Suez War</a>, 1967 <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">Six-Day War</a>, 1967-1970 <a href="/wiki/War_of_Attrition" title="War of Attrition">War of Attrition</a>, 1973 <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">Yom Kippur War</a>, <a href="/wiki/1982_Lebanon_War" title="1982 Lebanon War">1982 Lebanon War</a>, as well as a number of lesser conflicts of lower intensity.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Begin,_Carter_and_Sadat_at_Camp_David_1978.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="588" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Begin%2C_Carter_and_Sadat_at_Camp_David_1978.jpg/220px-Begin%2C_Carter_and_Sadat_at_Camp_David_1978.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Begin%2C_Carter_and_Sadat_at_Camp_David_1978.jpg/330px-Begin%2C_Carter_and_Sadat_at_Camp_David_1978.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Begin%2C_Carter_and_Sadat_at_Camp_David_1978.jpg/440px-Begin%2C_Carter_and_Sadat_at_Camp_David_1978.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Begin,_Carter_and_Sadat_at_Camp_David_1978.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Menachem_Begin" title="Menachem Begin">Menachem Begin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anwar_Sadat" title="Anwar Sadat">Anwar Sadat</a> concluded a peace treaty in 1978.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> between 1955 and 1974, conflict arising between <a href="/wiki/Greek_Cypriots" title="Greek Cypriots">Greek Cypriots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Cypriots" title="Turkish Cypriots">Turkish Cypriots</a> led to <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_intercommunal_violence" title="Cypriot intercommunal violence">Cypriot intercommunal violence</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_invasion_of_Cyprus" title="Turkish invasion of Cyprus">Turkish invasion of Cyprus</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyprus_dispute" title="Cyprus dispute">Cyprus dispute</a> remains unresolved.
</p><p>In the mid-to-late 1960s, the <a href="/wiki/Ba%27ath_Party" title="Ba'ath Party">Arab Socialist Ba'ath Party</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Michel_Aflaq" title="Michel Aflaq">Michel Aflaq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salah_al-Din_al-Bitar" title="Salah al-Din al-Bitar">Salah al-Din al-Bitar</a> took power in both Iraq and Syria. Iraq was first ruled by <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Hassan_al-Bakr" title="Ahmed Hassan al-Bakr">Ahmed Hassan al-Bakr</a>, but was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> in 1979, and Syria was ruled first by a Military Committee led by <a href="/wiki/Salah_Jadid" title="Salah Jadid">Salah Jadid</a>, and later <a href="/wiki/Hafez_al-Assad" title="Hafez al-Assad">Hafez al-Assad</a> until 2000, when he was succeeded by his son, <a href="/wiki/Bashar_al-Assad" title="Bashar al-Assad">Bashar al-Assad</a>.
</p><p>In 1979, Egypt under Nasser's successor, <a href="/wiki/Anwar_Sadat" title="Anwar Sadat">Anwar Sadat</a>, concluded a peace treaty with Israel, ending the prospects of a united Arab military front. From the 1970s the Palestinians, led by <a href="/wiki/Yasser_Arafat" title="Yasser Arafat">Yasser Arafat</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Palestine_Liberation_Organization" title="Palestine Liberation Organization">Palestine Liberation Organization</a>, resorted to a prolonged campaign of violence against Israel and against American, Jewish, and western targets generally, as a means of weakening Israeli resolve and undermining western support for Israel. The Palestinians were supported in this, to varying degrees, by the regimes in Syria, Libya, Iran, and Iraq. The high point of this campaign came in the 1975 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_Resolution_3379" title="United Nations General Assembly Resolution 3379">United Nations General Assembly Resolution 3379</a> condemning Zionism as a form of racism and the reception given to Arafat by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a>. Resolution 3379 was revoked in 1991 by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_Resolution_4686" title="United Nations General Assembly Resolution 4686">United Nations General Assembly Resolution 4686</a>.
</p><p>Due to many of the frantic events of the late 1970s in the Middle East it culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">Iran–Iraq War</a> between neighbouring Iran and Iraq. The war, started by Iraq, who invaded Iranian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khuzestan" title="Khuzestan">Khuzestan</a> in 1980 at the behest of the latter's chaotic state of country due to the 1979 Islamic Revolution, eventually turned into a stalemate with hundreds of thousands of dead on both sides.
</p><p>The fall of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and the collapse of <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a> in the early 1990s had several consequences for the Middle East. It allowed large numbers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_Jews" title="Soviet Jews">Soviet Jews</a> to emigrate from <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> to Israel, further strengthening the Jewish state. It cut off the easiest source of credit, armaments, and diplomatic support to the anti-western Arab regimes, weakening their position. It opened up the prospect of cheap oil from Russia, driving down the price of oil and reducing the west's dependence on oil from the Arab states. It discredited the model of development through authoritarian state socialism, which Egypt (under Nasser), Algeria, Syria, and Iraq had followed since the 1960s, leaving these regimes politically and economically stranded. Rulers such as Iraq's Saddam Hussein increasingly relied on <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">Arab nationalism</a> as a substitute for socialism.
</p><p>Saddam Hussein led Iraq into a prolonged and <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">costly war with Iran</a> from 1980 to 1988, and then into its fateful invasion of <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> in 1990. Kuwait had been part of the Ottoman province of <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a> before 1918, and thus in a sense part of Iraq, even though Iraq had recognized its <a href="/wiki/History_of_Kuwait#Independence_and_early_state-building_(1946–89)" title="History of Kuwait">independence in 1961</a>. In response, the United States formed a coalition of allies with Saudi Arabia, Egypt, and Syria, gained <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a> approval, and evicted Iraq from Kuwait by force in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>. President <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> did not, however, attempt to overthrow Saddam Hussein, which the United States later came to regret.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Gulf War led to a permanent U.S. military presence in the Persian Gulf, particularly in Saudi Arabia, which offended many Muslims, and was a reason often cited by <a href="/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden" title="Osama bin Laden">Osama bin Laden</a> as justification for the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="1990s.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1990s–present">1990s–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Middle_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: 1990s–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MiddleEast.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1662" data-file-width="1827" decoding="async" height="364" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/MiddleEast.png/400px-MiddleEast.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/MiddleEast.png/600px-MiddleEast.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/MiddleEast.png/800px-MiddleEast.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MiddleEast.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of the Middle East (2003)</div></div></div>
<p>The worldwide change of governance in Eastern Europe, Latin America, East Asia, and parts of Africa following the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolution of the Soviet Union</a> did not occur in the Middle East. In the whole region, only Israel, Turkey and to some extent Lebanon and the Palestinian territories were considered to be democracies. Some countries had legislative bodies, but these were said to have little power. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf_states" title="Persian Gulf states">Persian Gulf states</a> the majority of the population could not vote because they were guest workers rather than citizens.
</p><p>In most Middle Eastern countries, the growth of market economies was said to be limited by political restrictions, corruption, and <a href="/wiki/Cronyism" title="Cronyism">cronyism</a>, overspending on arms and prestige projects and over-dependence on oil revenues. The successful economies were countries that had oil wealth and low populations, such as <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, where the ruling emirs allowed some political and social liberalization, but without giving up any of their own power. Lebanon also rebuilt a fairly successful economy after a prolonged <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Civil_War" title="Lebanese Civil War">civil war</a> in the 1980s.
</p><p>At the beginning of the 21st century, all these factors intensified conflict in the Middle East, which affected the entire world. <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>'s failed attempt to broker a peace deal between Israel and Palestine at the <a href="/wiki/2000_Camp_David_Summit" title="2000 Camp David Summit">Camp David Summit</a> in 2000 led directly to the election of <a href="/wiki/Ariel_Sharon" title="Ariel Sharon">Ariel Sharon</a> as Prime Minister of Israel and to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Intifada" title="Second Intifada">Second Intifada</a>, which conducted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suicide_bombing" title="Suicide bombing">suicide bombings</a> on Israeli civilians. This was the first major outbreak of violence since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oslo_Peace_Accords" title="Oslo Peace Accords">Oslo Peace Accords</a> of 1993.
</p><p>At the same time, the failures of most of the Arab governments and the bankruptcy of secular Arab radicalism led a section of educated Arabs (and other Muslims) to embrace <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamism</a>, promoted both by Iran's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shi%27a" title="Shi'a">Shi'a</a> clerics as well as by Saudi Arabia's powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wahhabist" title="Wahhabist">Wahhabist</a> sect. Many of the militant Islamists gained their military training while <a href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War" title="Soviet–Afghan War">fighting Soviet forces</a> in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (July 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:History_of_the_Middle_East#Dubious" title="Talk:History of the Middle East">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> Many of the <a href="/wiki/Mujahideen" title="Mujahideen">Afghan jihadists</a>, though supposedly none of the Arab volunteers, were funded by the United States under <a href="/wiki/Operation_Cyclone" title="Operation Cyclone">Operation Cyclone</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Reagan_Doctrine" title="Reagan Doctrine">Reagan Doctrine</a>, one of the longest and most expensive <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covert_operations" title="Covert operations">covert operations</a> ever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergen68_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-bergen68-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oily_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oily-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of these Arab militants was a wealthy Saudi Arabian named <a href="/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden" title="Osama bin Laden">Osama bin Laden</a>. After fighting against the <a href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War" title="Soviet–Afghan War">Soviets in Afghanistan</a> in the 1980s, he formed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Qaida" title="Al-Qaida">al-Qaida</a> organization, which was responsible for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1998_U.S._embassy_bombings" title="1998 U.S. embassy bombings">1998 U.S. embassy bombings</a>, the <a href="/wiki/USS_Cole_bombing" title="USS Cole bombing">USS <i>Cole</i> bombing</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" title="September 11, 2001 attacks">September 11, 2001 attacks</a> on the United States.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The September 11 attacks led the <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> administration to <a href="/wiki/United_States_invasion_of_Afghanistan" title="United States invasion of Afghanistan">invade Afghanistan</a> in 2001 to overthrow the <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a> regime, which had been harbouring Bin Laden and al-Qaida. The United States and its allies described this operation as part of a global "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" title="War on Terror">War on Terror</a>".
</p><p>In 2002, U.S. Defense Secretary <a href="/wiki/Donald_Rumsfeld" title="Donald Rumsfeld">Donald Rumsfeld</a> developed a plan to invade Iraq, remove Saddam from power, and turn Iraq into a democratic state with a free-market economy, which they hoped would serve as a model for the rest of the Middle East. The United States and its principal allies—Britain, Italy, Spain, and Australia—could not secure <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> approval for the execution of the numerous UN resolutions, so they launched an invasion of Iraq and deposed Saddam without much difficulty in April 2003.
</p><p>The advent of a new western army of occupation in a Middle Eastern capital marked a turning point in the history of the region. Despite successful elections (although boycotted by large portions of Iraq's Sunni population) held in January 2005, much of Iraq had all but disintegrated, due to a post-war insurgency which morphed into persistent ethnic violence that the American army was initially unable to quell. Many of Iraq's intellectual and business elite fled the country, and many Iraqi refugees left as a result of the insurgency, further destabilizing the region. A responsive surge in U.S. forces in Iraq was largely successful in controlling the insurgency and stabilizing the country. U.S. forces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Withdrawal_of_U.S._troops_from_Iraq_(2007%E2%80%932011)" title="Withdrawal of U.S. troops from Iraq (2007–2011)">withdrew</a> from Iraq by December 2011.
</p><p>By 2005, President George W. Bush's <a href="/wiki/Road_map_for_peace" title="Road map for peace">Road map for peace</a> between Israel and the Palestinians was stalled, although this situation had begun to change with <a href="/wiki/Yasser_Arafat" title="Yasser Arafat">Yasser Arafat</a>'s death in 2004. In response, Israel moved towards a unilateral solution, pushing ahead with the <a href="/wiki/Israeli_West_Bank_barrier" title="Israeli West Bank barrier">Israeli West Bank barrier</a> to protect Israel from Palestinian suicide bombers and proposed unilateral withdrawal from <a href="/wiki/Gaza_Strip" title="Gaza Strip">Gaza</a>. In 2006 a new conflict erupted between Israel and <a href="/wiki/Hezbollah" title="Hezbollah">Hezbollah</a> Shi'a militia in southern Lebanon, further setting back any "prospects for peace".
</p><p>In the early 2010s, a <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_wave" title="Revolutionary wave">revolutionary wave</a> popularly known as the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a> brought major protests, uprisings, and revolutions to several Middle Eastern countries, followed by prolonged civil wars in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" title="Syrian Civil War">Syria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iraqi_Civil_War_(2014%E2%80%932017)" title="Iraqi Civil War (2014–2017)">Iraq</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yemeni_Civil_War_(2015%E2%80%93present)" title="Yemeni Civil War (2015–present)">Yemen</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Libyan_Civil_War_(2011%E2%80%93present)" title="Libyan Civil War (2011–present)">Libya</a>. In 2014, a terrorist group and self-proclaimed caliphate calling itself the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State</a> made rapid territorial gains in western Iraq and eastern Syria, prompting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_military_intervention_against_ISIL" title="International military intervention against ISIL">international military intervention</a>. At its peak, the group controlled an area containing an estimated 2.8 to 8 million people, 98% of which was lost by December 2017.
</p>

Routledge. p. 33. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781135980795" title="Special:BookSources/9781135980795"><bdi>9781135980795</bdi></a>. <q>Most Druze do not consider themselves Muslim. Historically they faced much persecution and keep their religious beliefs secrets.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Middle+East+Today%3A+Political%2C+Geographical+and+Cultural+Perspectives&amp;rft.pages=33&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.isbn=9781135980795&amp;rft.aulast=J.+Stewart&amp;rft.aufirst=Dona&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHistory+of+the+Middle+East"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFYazbeck_Haddad2014">Yazbeck Haddad, Yvonne (2014). <i>The Oxford Handbook of American Islam</i>. Oxford University Press. p. 142. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780199862634" title="Special:BookSources/9780199862634"><bdi>9780199862634</bdi></a>. <q>While they appear parallel to those of normative Islam, in the Druze religion they are different in meaning and interpretation. The religion is consider distinct from the Ismaili as well as from other Muslims belief and practice... Most Druze consider themselves fully assimilated in American society and do not necessarily identify as Muslims..</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Oxford+Handbook+of+American+Islam&amp;rft.pages=142&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.isbn=9780199862634&amp;rft.aulast=Yazbeck+Haddad&amp;rft.aufirst=Yvonne&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHistory+of+the+Middle+East"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-De_McLaurin_1979_114-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-De_McLaurin_1979_114_12-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFDe_McLaurin1979">De McLaurin, Ronald (1979). <i>The Political Role of Minority Groups in the Middle East</i>. Michigan University Press. p. 114. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780030525964" title="Special:BookSources/9780030525964"><bdi>9780030525964</bdi></a>. <q>Theologically, one would have to conclude that the Druze are not Muslims. They do not accept the five pillars of Islam. In place of these principles the Druze have instituted the seven precepts noted above..</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Political+Role+of+Minority+Groups+in+the+Middle+East&amp;rft.pages=114&amp;rft.pub=Michigan+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1979&amp;rft.isbn=9780030525964&amp;rft.aulast=De+McLaurin&amp;rft.aufirst=Ronald&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHistory+of+the+Middle+East"></span></span>
